   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Modernism
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="hatnote">
      For other uses of the word, see
      <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Modernism_(disambiguation)" title="Modernism (disambiguation)">
       Modernism (disambiguation)
      </a>
      .  For the period in sociology beginning with the industrialization, see
      <a href="/wiki/Modernity" title="Modernity">
       Modernity
      </a>
      .
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote">
      Related terms are
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_(disambiguation)" title="Modern (disambiguation)">
        modern
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       modernist
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contemporary" title="Contemporary">
        contemporary
       </a>
      </i>
      , and
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postmodern" title="Postmodern">
        postmodern
       </a>
      </i>
      .
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hans_Hofmann%27s_painting_%27The_Gate%27,_1959%E2%80%9360.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="573" data-file-width="368" height="343" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9b/Hans_Hofmann%27s_painting_%27The_Gate%27%2C_1959%E2%80%9360.jpg/220px-Hans_Hofmann%27s_painting_%27The_Gate%27%2C_1959%E2%80%9360.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9b/Hans_Hofmann%27s_painting_%27The_Gate%27%2C_1959%E2%80%9360.jpg/330px-Hans_Hofmann%27s_painting_%27The_Gate%27%2C_1959%E2%80%9360.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/9/9b/Hans_Hofmann%27s_painting_%27The_Gate%27%2C_1959%E2%80%9360.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hans_Hofmann%27s_painting_%27The_Gate%27,_1959%E2%80%9360.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Hans_Hofmann" title="Hans Hofmann">
         Hans Hofmann
        </a>
        , "The Gate", 19591960, collection:
        <a href="/wiki/Solomon_R._Guggenheim_Museum" title="Solomon R. Guggenheim Museum">
         Solomon R. Guggenheim Museum
        </a>
        . Hofmann was renowned not only as an artist but also as a teacher of art, and a modernist theorist both in his native Germany and later in the U.S. During the 1930s in New York and California he introduced Modernism and modernist theories to a new generation of American artists. Through his teaching and his lectures at his art schools in
        <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Village" title="Greenwich Village">
         Greenwich Village
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Provincetown,_Massachusetts" title="Provincetown, Massachusetts">
         Provincetown
        </a>
        , Massachusetts, he widened the scope of Modernism in the United States.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-1">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          1
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <b>
       Modernism
      </b>
      is a philosophical movement that, along with cultural trends and changes, arose from wide-scale and far-reaching transformations in
      <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">
       Western society
      </a>
      in the late 19th and early 20th centuries. Among the factors that shaped Modernism were the development of modern industrial societies and the rapid growth of cities, followed then by the horror of
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
       World War I
      </a>
      . Modernism also rejected the certainty of
      <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">
       Enlightenment
      </a>
      thinking, and many modernists rejected religious belief.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Modernism, in general, includes the activities and creations of those who felt the traditional forms of art, architecture, literature, religious faith, philosophy, social organization, activities of daily life, and even the sciences, were becoming ill-fitted to their tasks and outdated in the new economic, social, and political environment of an emerging fully industrialized world. The poet
      <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Pound" title="Ezra Pound">
       Ezra Pound
      </a>
      's 1934 injunction to "Make it new!" was the touchstone of the movement's approach towards what it saw as the now obsolete culture of the past. In this spirit, its innovations, like the
      <a href="/wiki/Stream_of_consciousness_(narrative_mode)" title="Stream of consciousness (narrative mode)">
       stream-of-consciousness
      </a>
      novel,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atonal" title="Atonal">
       atonal
      </a>
      (or pantonal) and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelve-tone" title="Twelve-tone">
       twelve-tone
      </a>
      music,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divisionist" title="Divisionist">
       divisionist
      </a>
      painting and
      <a href="/wiki/Abstract_art" title="Abstract art">
       abstract art
      </a>
      , all had precursors in the 19th century.
     </p>
     <p>
      A notable characteristic of Modernism is self-consciousness, which often led to experiments with form, along with the use of techniques that drew attention to the processes and materials used in creating a painting, poem, building, etc.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Modernism explicitly rejected the ideology of
      <a href="/wiki/Realism_(arts)" title="Realism (arts)">
       realism
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barth79Replenishment_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Barth79Replenishment-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graff73_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Graff73-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graff75_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Graff75-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and makes use of the works of the past by the employment of
      <a href="/wiki/Reprise" title="Reprise">
       reprise
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Incorporation_(linguistics)" title="Incorporation (linguistics)">
       incorporation
      </a>
      , rewriting,
      <a href="/wiki/Recapitulation_(music)" title="Recapitulation (music)">
       recapitulation
      </a>
      , revision and
      <a href="/wiki/Parody" title="Parody">
       parody
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eco90_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Eco90-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steiner98p489_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Steiner98p489-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Childs2000p17_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Childs2000p17-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Some commentators define Modernism as a mode of thinkingone or more philosophically defined characteristics, like self-consciousness or self-reference, that run across all the novelties in the arts and the disciplines.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      More common, especially in the West, are those who see it as a socially progressive trend of thought that affirms the power of human beings to create, improve and reshape their environment with the aid of practical experimentation, scientific knowledge, or technology.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      From this perspective, Modernism encouraged the re-examination of every aspect of existence, from commerce to philosophy, with the goal of finding that which was 'holding back' progress, and replacing it with new ways of reaching the same end. Others focus on Modernism as an aesthetic introspection. This facilitates consideration of specific reactions to the use of technology in the First World War, and anti-technological and nihilistic aspects of the works of diverse thinkers and artists spanning the period from
      <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">
       Friedrich Nietzsche
      </a>
      (18441900) to
      <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Beckett" title="Samuel Beckett">
       Samuel Beckett
      </a>
      (19061989).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

         <span class="toctext">
          External links
         </span>
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
     </div>
     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modernism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Beginnings:_the_19th_century">
       Beginnings: the 19th century
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modernism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Beginnings: the 19th century">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_La_libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2480" data-file-width="3133" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_La_libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple.jpg/200px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_La_libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_La_libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple.jpg/300px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_La_libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_La_libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple.jpg/400px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_La_libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_La_libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix" title="Eugne Delacroix">
         Eugne Delacroix
        </a>
        's
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Leading_the_People" title="Liberty Leading the People">
          Liberty Leading the People
         </a>
        </i>
        , 1830, a
        <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">
         Romantic
        </a>
        work of art
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div style="width:52px">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="204" data-file-width="262" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
         </a>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text">
        <span class="mbox-text-span">
         This section
         <b>
          needs additional citations for
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
           verification
          </a>
         </b>
         .
         <span class="hide-when-compact">
          Please help
          <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Modernism&amp;action=edit">
           improve this article
          </a>
          by
          <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing/1">
           adding citations to reliable sources
          </a>
          . Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.
         </span>
         <small>
          <i>
           (January 2013)
          </i>
         </small>
        </span>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      According to one critic, modernism developed out of
      <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">
       Romanticism
      </a>
      's revolt against the effects of the
      <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">
       Industrial Revolution
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bourgeois" title="Bourgeois">
       bourgeois
      </a>
      values: "The ground motive of modernism, Graff asserts, was criticism of the nineteenth-century bourgeois social order and its world view [] the modernists, carrying the torch of romanticism".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barth79Replenishment_5-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Barth79Replenishment-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graff73_6-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Graff73-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graff75_7-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Graff75-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While
      <a href="/wiki/J._M._W._Turner" title="J. M. W. Turner">
       J. M. W. Turner
      </a>
      (17751851), one of the greatest landscape painters of the 19th century, was a member of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romantic_movement" title="Romantic movement">
       Romantic movement
      </a>
      , as "a pioneer in the study of light, colour, and atmosphere", he "anticipated the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Impressionists" title="French Impressionists">
       French Impressionists
      </a>
      " and therefore Modernism "in breaking down conventional formulas of representation; [though] unlike them, he believed that his works should always express significant historical, mythological, literary, or other narrative themes".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Franz_von_Lenbach_Bismarck.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="534" data-file-width="439" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Franz_von_Lenbach_Bismarck.jpg/180px-Franz_von_Lenbach_Bismarck.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Franz_von_Lenbach_Bismarck.jpg/270px-Franz_von_Lenbach_Bismarck.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Franz_von_Lenbach_Bismarck.jpg/360px-Franz_von_Lenbach_Bismarck.jpg 2x" width="180"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Franz_von_Lenbach_Bismarck.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A
        <a href="/wiki/Realism_(arts)" title="Realism (arts)">
         Realist
        </a>
        portrait of
        <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">
         Otto von Bismarck
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The dominant trends of industrial
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victorian_England" title="Victorian England">
       Victorian England
      </a>
      , were also opposed, from about 1850, by the English poets and painters that constituted the
      <a href="/wiki/Pre-Raphaelite_Brotherhood" title="Pre-Raphaelite Brotherhood">
       Pre-Raphaelite Brotherhood
      </a>
      , because of their "opposition to technical skill without inspiration".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They were influenced by the writings of the art critic
      <a href="/wiki/John_Ruskin" title="John Ruskin">
       John Ruskin
      </a>
      (18191900), who had strong feelings about the role of art in helping to improve the lives of the urban working classes, in the rapidly expanding industrial cities of Britain.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Art critic
      <a href="/wiki/Clement_Greenberg" title="Clement Greenberg">
       Clement Greenberg
      </a>
      describes the Pre-Raphaelite Brotherhood as proto-Modernists: "There the proto-Modernists were, of all people, the pre-Raphaelites (and even before them, as proto-proto-Modernists, the German
      <a href="/wiki/Nazarene_movement" title="Nazarene movement">
       Nazarenes
      </a>
      . The Pre-Raphaelites actually foreshadowed
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manet" title="Manet">
       Manet
      </a>
      (183283), with whom Modernist painting most definitely begins. They acted on a dissatisfaction with painting as practiced in their time, holding that its realism wasn't truthful enough".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">
       Rationalism
      </a>
      has also had opponents in the philosophers
      <a href="/wiki/S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard" title="Sren Kierkegaard">
       Sren Kierkegaard
      </a>
      (181355)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanford_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stanford-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and later
      <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">
       Friedrich Nietzsche
      </a>
      (18441900), both of whom had significant influence on
      <a href="/wiki/Existentialism" title="Existentialism">
       existentialism
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      However, the Industrial Revolution continued. Influential innovations included steam-powered industrialization, and especially the development of railways, starting in Britain in the 1830s,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the subsequent advancements in physics, engineering, and architecture associated with this. A major 19th-century engineering achievement was
      <a href="/wiki/The_Crystal_Palace" title="The Crystal Palace">
       The Crystal Palace
      </a>
      , the huge cast-iron and plate glass exhibition hall built for
      <a href="/wiki/The_Great_Exhibition" title="The Great Exhibition">
       The Great Exhibition
      </a>
      of 1851 in London. Glass and iron were used in a similar monumental style in the construction of major railway terminals in
      <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">
       London
      </a>
      , such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paddington_Station" title="Paddington Station">
       Paddington Station
      </a>
      (1854)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King%27s_Cross_Station" title="King's Cross Station">
       King's Cross Station
      </a>
      (1852).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These technological advances led to the building of later structures like the
      <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Bridge" title="Brooklyn Bridge">
       Brooklyn Bridge
      </a>
      (1883) and the
      <a href="/wiki/Eiffel_Tower" title="Eiffel Tower">
       Eiffel Tower
      </a>
      (1889). The latter broke all previous limitations on how tall man-made objects could be. These engineering marvels radically altered the 19th-century urban environment and the daily lives of people. The human experience of time itself was altered, with the development of electric
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telegraph" title="Telegraph">
       telegraph
      </a>
      from 1837,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the adoption of
      <a href="/wiki/Standard_time" title="Standard time">
       standard time
      </a>
      by British railway companies from 1845, and in the rest of the world over the next fifty years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      But despite continuing technological advances, from the 1870s onward, the idea that history and civilization were inherently progressive, and that progress was always good, came under increasing attack. Arguments arose that the values of the artist and those of society were not merely different, but that Society was antithetical to Progress, and could not move forward in its present form. The philosopher
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schopenhauer" title="Schopenhauer">
       Schopenhauer
      </a>
      (17881860) (
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_World_as_Will_and_Idea" title="The World as Will and Idea">
        The World as Will and Idea
       </a>
      </i>
      , 1819) called into question the previous optimism, and his ideas had an important influence on later thinkers, including Nietzsche.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanford_18-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stanford-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Two of the most significant thinkers of the period were biologist
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">
       Charles Darwin
      </a>
      (180982), author of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/On_the_Origin_of_Species" title="On the Origin of Species">
        On the Origin of Species by Means of Natural Selection
       </a>
      </i>
      (1859), and political scientist
      <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">
       Karl Marx
      </a>
      (181883), author of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Das_Kapital" title="Das Kapital">
        Das Kapital
       </a>
      </i>
      (1867). Darwin's theory of evolution by natural selection undermined religious certainty and the idea of human uniqueness. In particular, the notion that human beings were driven by the same impulses as "lower animals" proved to be difficult to reconcile with the idea of an ennobling spirituality.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Karl Marx argued that there were fundamental contradictions within the capitalist system, and that the workers were anything but free.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Redon_spirit-waters.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1028" data-file-width="831" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Redon_spirit-waters.jpg/220px-Redon_spirit-waters.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Redon_spirit-waters.jpg/330px-Redon_spirit-waters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Redon_spirit-waters.jpg/440px-Redon_spirit-waters.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Redon_spirit-waters.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Odilon_Redon" title="Odilon Redon">
         Odilon Redon
        </a>
        ,
        <i>
         Guardian Spirit of the Waters
        </i>
        , 1878, charcoal on paper,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Art_Institute_of_Chicago" title="The Art Institute of Chicago">
         The Art Institute of Chicago
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="The_beginnings_of_modernism_in_France">
       The beginnings of modernism in France
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modernism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: The beginnings of modernism in France">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Historians, and writers in different disciplines, have suggested various dates as starting points for modernism. Historian
      <a href="/wiki/William_Everdell" title="William Everdell">
       William Everdell
      </a>
      , for example, has argued that Modernism began in the 1870s, when metaphorical (or ontological) continuity began to yield to the discrete with mathematician
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dedekind" title="Richard Dedekind">
       Richard Dedekind
      </a>
      's (18311916)
      <a href="/wiki/Dedekind_cut" title="Dedekind cut">
       Dedekind cut
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Boltzmann" title="Ludwig Boltzmann">
       Ludwig Boltzmann
      </a>
      's (18441906)
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Statistical_thermodynamics" title="Statistical thermodynamics">
       statistical thermodynamics
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Everdell also thinks Modernism in painting began in 188586 with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seurat" title="Seurat">
       Seurat
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/Divisionism" title="Divisionism">
       Divisionism
      </a>
      , the "dots" used to paint "
      <a href="/wiki/A_Sunday_Afternoon_on_the_Island_of_La_Grande_Jatte" title="A Sunday Afternoon on the Island of La Grande Jatte">
       A Sunday Afternoon on the Island of La Grande Jatte
      </a>
      ." On the other hand visual art critic
      <a href="/wiki/Clement_Greenberg" title="Clement Greenberg">
       Clement Greenberg
      </a>
      called
      <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">
       Immanuel Kant
      </a>
      (17241804) "the first real Modernist",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      though he also wrote, "What can be safely called Modernism emerged in the middle of the last centuryand rather locally, in France, with
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_Baudelaire" title="Charles Baudelaire">
       Baudelaire
      </a>
      in literature and
      <a href="/wiki/%C3%89douard_Manet" title="douard Manet">
       Manet
      </a>
      in painting, and perhaps with
      <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Flaubert" title="Gustave Flaubert">
       Flaubert
      </a>
      , too, in prose fiction. (It was a while later, and not so locally, that Modernism appeared in
      <a href="/wiki/Modernism_(music)" title="Modernism (music)">
       music
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Modern_architecture" title="Modern architecture">
       architecture
      </a>
      )."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The poet Baudelaire's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Les_Fleurs_du_mal" title="Les Fleurs du mal">
        Les Fleurs du mal
       </a>
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       The Flowers of Evil
      </i>
      ), and Flaubert's novel
      <a href="/wiki/Madame_Bovary" title="Madame Bovary">
       Madame Bovary
      </a>
      were both published in 1857.
     </p>
     <p>
      In the arts and letters, two important approaches developed separately in France. The first was
      <a href="/wiki/Impressionism" title="Impressionism">
       impressionism
      </a>
      , a school of painting that initially focused on work done, not in studios, but outdoors (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/En_plein_air" title="En plein air">
        en plein air
       </a>
      </i>
      ). Impressionist paintings demonstrated that human beings do not see objects, but instead see light itself. The school gathered adherents despite internal divisions among its leading practitioners, and became increasingly influential. Initially rejected from the most important commercial show of the time, the government-sponsored
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paris_Salon" title="Paris Salon">
       Paris Salon
      </a>
      , the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Impressionists" title="Impressionists">
       Impressionists
      </a>
      organized yearly group exhibitions in commercial venues during the 1870s and 1880s, timing them to coincide with the official Salon. A significant event of 1863 was the
      <a href="/wiki/Salon_des_Refus%C3%A9s" title="Salon des Refuss">
       Salon des Refuss
      </a>
      , created by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_III_of_France" title="Napoleon III of France">
       Emperor NapoleonIII
      </a>
      to display all of the paintings rejected by the Paris Salon. While most were in standard styles, but by inferior artists, the work of
      <a href="/wiki/%C3%89douard_Manet" title="douard Manet">
       Manet
      </a>
      attracted tremendous attention, and opened commercial doors to the movement. The second French school was
      <a href="/wiki/Symbolism_(arts)" title="Symbolism (arts)">
       Symbolism
      </a>
      , which literary historians see beginning with Charles Baudelaire (182167), and including the later poets,
      <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Rimbaud" title="Arthur Rimbaud">
       Arthur Rimbaud
      </a>
      (185491)
      <i>
       Une Saison en Enfer
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       A Season in Hell
      </i>
      , 1873),
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_Verlaine" title="Paul Verlaine">
       Paul Verlaine
      </a>
      (184496),
      <a href="/wiki/St%C3%A9phane_Mallarm%C3%A9" title="Stphane Mallarm">
       Stphane Mallarm
      </a>
      (184298), and
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_Val%C3%A9ry" title="Paul Valry">
       Paul Valry
      </a>
      (18711945). The symbolists "stressed the priority of suggestion and evocation over direct description and explicit analogy," and were especially interested in "the musical properties of language."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Cabaret" title="Cabaret">
       Cabaret
      </a>
      , which gave birth to so many of the arts of modernism, including the immediate precursors of film, may be said to have begun in France in 1881 with the opening of the
      <a href="/wiki/Le_Chat_Noir" title="Le Chat Noir">
       Black Cat
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Montmartre" title="Montmartre">
       Montmartre
      </a>
      , the beginning of the ironic monologue, and the founding of the Society of Incoherent Arts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bonheur_Matisse.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="784" data-file-width="1088" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b9/Bonheur_Matisse.jpg/220px-Bonheur_Matisse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b9/Bonheur_Matisse.jpg/330px-Bonheur_Matisse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b9/Bonheur_Matisse.jpg/440px-Bonheur_Matisse.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bonheur_Matisse.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Henri_Matisse" title="Henri Matisse">
         Henri Matisse
        </a>
        ,
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Le_bonheur_de_vivre" title="Le bonheur de vivre">
          Le bonheur de vivre
         </a>
        </i>
        , 1905-6,
        <a href="/wiki/Barnes_Foundation" title="Barnes Foundation">
         Barnes Foundation
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merion,_PA" title="Merion, PA">
         Merion,PA
        </a>
        . An early
        <a href="/wiki/Fauvism" title="Fauvism">
         Fauvist
        </a>
        masterpiece
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4661" data-file-width="4500" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon.jpg/220px-Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon.jpg/330px-Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon.jpg/440px-Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Picasso" title="Pablo Picasso">
         Pablo Picasso
        </a>
        ,
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon" title="Les Demoiselles d'Avignon">
          Les Demoiselles d'Avignon
         </a>
        </i>
        (1907), is considered to have re-invented the art of painting. Many of Picasso's friends and colleagues, even fellow painters
        <a href="/wiki/Henri_Matisse" title="Henri Matisse">
         Henri Matisse
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Georges_Braque" title="Georges Braque">
         Georges Braque
        </a>
        , were upset when they saw this painting.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Influential in the early days of Modernism were the theories of
      <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">
       Sigmund Freud
      </a>
      (18561939). Freud's first major work was
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Studies_on_Hysteria" title="Studies on Hysteria">
        Studies on Hysteria
       </a>
      </i>
      (with
      <a href="/wiki/Josef_Breuer" title="Josef Breuer">
       Josef Breuer
      </a>
      ) (1895). Central to Freud's thinking is the idea "of the primacy of the unconscious mind in mental life", so that all subjective reality was based on the play of basic drives and instincts, through which the outside world was perceived. Freud's description of subjective states involved an unconscious mind full of primal impulses, and counterbalancing self-imposed restrictions derived from social values.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">
       Friedrich Nietzsche
      </a>
      (18441900) was another major precursor of modernism,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      with a philosophy in which psychological drives, specifically the '
      <a href="/wiki/Will_to_power" title="Will to power">
       will to power
      </a>
      ', was of central importance: "Nietzsche often identified life itself with will to power, that is, with an instinct for growth and durability".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Henri_Bergson" title="Henri Bergson">
       Henri Bergson
      </a>
      (18591941), on the other hand, emphasized the difference between scientific, clock time and the direct, subjective, human experience of time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      His work on time and consciousness "had a great influence on twentieth-century novelists," especially those modernists who used the
      <a href="/wiki/Stream_of_consciousness_(narrative_mode)" title="Stream of consciousness (narrative mode)">
       stream of consciousness
      </a>
      technique, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Richardson" title="Dorothy Richardson">
       Dorothy Richardson
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/James_Joyce" title="James Joyce">
       James Joyce
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Woolf" title="Virginia Woolf">
       Virginia Woolf
      </a>
      (18821941).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Also important in Bergson's philosophy was the idea of
      <i>
       lan vital
      </i>
      , the life force, which "brings about the creative evolution of everything".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collinson.2C_132_38-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Collinson.2C_132-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      His philosophy also placed a high value on intuition, though without rejecting the importance of the intellect.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collinson.2C_132_38-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Collinson.2C_132-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Important literary precursors of Modernism were:
      <a href="/wiki/Fyodor_Dostoyevsky" title="Fyodor Dostoyevsky">
       Fyodor Dostoyevsky
      </a>
      (182181)
      <i>
       Crime and Punishment
      </i>
      (1866),
      <i>
       The Brothers Karamazov
      </i>
      (1880);
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Walt_Whitman" title="Walt Whitman">
       Walt Whitman
      </a>
      (181992) (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Leaves_of_Grass" title="Leaves of Grass">
        Leaves of Grass
       </a>
      </i>
      ) (185591);
      <a href="/wiki/August_Strindberg" title="August Strindberg">
       August Strindberg
      </a>
      (18491912), especially his later plays, including, the trilogy
      <i>
       To Damascus
      </i>
      18981901,
      <i>
       A Dream Play
      </i>
      (1902),
      <i>
       The Ghost Sonata
      </i>
      (1907).
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_James" title="Henry James">
       Henry James
      </a>
      has also been suggested as a significant precursor, in a work as early as
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portrait_of_a_Lady" title="Portrait of a Lady">
        Portrait of a Lady
       </a>
      </i>
      (1881).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Out of the collision of ideals derived from Romanticism, and an attempt to find a way for knowledge to explain that which was as yet unknown, came the first wave of works in the first decade of the 20th century, which, while their authors considered them extensions of existing trends in art, broke the implicit contract with the general public that artists were the interpreters and representatives of bourgeois culture and ideas. These "modernist" landmarks include the
      <a href="/wiki/Atonality" title="Atonality">
       atonal
      </a>
      ending of
      <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Schoenberg" title="Arnold Schoenberg">
       Arnold Schoenberg
      </a>
      's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/String_quartets_(Schoenberg)" title="String quartets (Schoenberg)">
       Second String Quartet
      </a>
      in 1908, the
      <a href="/wiki/Expressionism" title="Expressionism">
       expressionist
      </a>
      paintings of
      <a href="/wiki/Wassily_Kandinsky" title="Wassily Kandinsky">
       Wassily Kandinsky
      </a>
      starting in 1903, and culminating with his first abstract painting and the founding of the
      <a href="/wiki/Der_Blaue_Reiter" title="Der Blaue Reiter">
       Blue Rider
      </a>
      group in
      <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">
       Munich
      </a>
      in 1911, and the rise of
      <a href="/wiki/Fauvism" title="Fauvism">
       fauvism
      </a>
      and the inventions of
      <a href="/wiki/Cubism" title="Cubism">
       cubism
      </a>
      from the studios of
      <a href="/wiki/Henri_Matisse" title="Henri Matisse">
       Henri Matisse
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Picasso" title="Pablo Picasso">
       Pablo Picasso
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Georges_Braque" title="Georges Braque">
       Georges Braque
      </a>
      , and others, in the years between 1900 and 1910.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Explosion.2C_early_20th_century_to_1930">
       Explosion, early 20th century to 1930
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modernism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Explosion, early 20th century to 1930">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Picasso_Portrait_of_Daniel-Henry_Kahnweiler_1910.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="528" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/68/Picasso_Portrait_of_Daniel-Henry_Kahnweiler_1910.jpg/170px-Picasso_Portrait_of_Daniel-Henry_Kahnweiler_1910.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/68/Picasso_Portrait_of_Daniel-Henry_Kahnweiler_1910.jpg/255px-Picasso_Portrait_of_Daniel-Henry_Kahnweiler_1910.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/68/Picasso_Portrait_of_Daniel-Henry_Kahnweiler_1910.jpg/340px-Picasso_Portrait_of_Daniel-Henry_Kahnweiler_1910.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Picasso_Portrait_of_Daniel-Henry_Kahnweiler_1910.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Pablo Picasso,
        <i>
         Portrait of
         <a href="/wiki/Daniel-Henry_Kahnweiler" title="Daniel-Henry Kahnweiler">
          Daniel-Henry Kahnweiler
         </a>
        </i>
        , 1910,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Art_Institute_of_Chicago" title="The Art Institute of Chicago">
         The Art Institute of Chicago
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      An important aspect of Modernism is how it relates to tradition through its adoption of techniques like reprise, incorporation, rewriting, recapitulation, revision and parody in new forms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eco90_8-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Eco90-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steiner98p489_9-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Steiner98p489-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Piet_Mondrian,_1909,_View_from_the_Dunes_with_Beach_and_Piers,_Domburg,_MoMA.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="371" data-file-width="500" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Piet_Mondrian%2C_1909%2C_View_from_the_Dunes_with_Beach_and_Piers%2C_Domburg%2C_MoMA.jpg/220px-Piet_Mondrian%2C_1909%2C_View_from_the_Dunes_with_Beach_and_Piers%2C_Domburg%2C_MoMA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Piet_Mondrian%2C_1909%2C_View_from_the_Dunes_with_Beach_and_Piers%2C_Domburg%2C_MoMA.jpg/330px-Piet_Mondrian%2C_1909%2C_View_from_the_Dunes_with_Beach_and_Piers%2C_Domburg%2C_MoMA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Piet_Mondrian%2C_1909%2C_View_from_the_Dunes_with_Beach_and_Piers%2C_Domburg%2C_MoMA.jpg/440px-Piet_Mondrian%2C_1909%2C_View_from_the_Dunes_with_Beach_and_Piers%2C_Domburg%2C_MoMA.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Piet_Mondrian,_1909,_View_from_the_Dunes_with_Beach_and_Piers,_Domburg,_MoMA.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Piet_Mondrian" title="Piet Mondrian">
         Piet Mondrian
        </a>
        ,
        <i>
         View from the Dunes with Beach and Piers, Domburg,
        </i>
        1909, oil and pencil on cardboard,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Museum_of_Modern_Art,_New_York_City" title="Museum of Modern Art, New York City">
         Museum of Modern Art, New York City
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Museum_Reina_Sof%C3%ADa_Madrid_Spain_Espana_this_is_a_front_photo_close_up_at_the_Queen_Sofia_Museum_in_2011_month_of_June.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2736" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Museum_Reina_Sof%C3%ADa_Madrid_Spain_Espana_this_is_a_front_photo_close_up_at_the_Queen_Sofia_Museum_in_2011_month_of_June.jpg/220px-Museum_Reina_Sof%C3%ADa_Madrid_Spain_Espana_this_is_a_front_photo_close_up_at_the_Queen_Sofia_Museum_in_2011_month_of_June.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Museum_Reina_Sof%C3%ADa_Madrid_Spain_Espana_this_is_a_front_photo_close_up_at_the_Queen_Sofia_Museum_in_2011_month_of_June.jpg/330px-Museum_Reina_Sof%C3%ADa_Madrid_Spain_Espana_this_is_a_front_photo_close_up_at_the_Queen_Sofia_Museum_in_2011_month_of_June.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Museum_Reina_Sof%C3%ADa_Madrid_Spain_Espana_this_is_a_front_photo_close_up_at_the_Queen_Sofia_Museum_in_2011_month_of_June.jpg/440px-Museum_Reina_Sof%C3%ADa_Madrid_Spain_Espana_this_is_a_front_photo_close_up_at_the_Queen_Sofia_Museum_in_2011_month_of_June.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Museum_Reina_Sof%C3%ADa_Madrid_Spain_Espana_this_is_a_front_photo_close_up_at_the_Queen_Sofia_Museum_in_2011_month_of_June.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Museo_Nacional_Centro_de_Arte_Reina_Sof%C3%ADa" title="Museo Nacional Centro de Arte Reina Sofa">
         The Museo Nacional Centro de Arte Reina Sofa
        </a>
        (MNCARS) is the official name of Spain's national museum of 20th-century art, located in
        <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">
         Madrid
        </a>
        (informally shortened to the Museo Reina Sofa, Queen Sofia Museum). The photo shows the old building with the addition of one of the contemporary glass towers to the exterior by
        <a href="/wiki/Ian_Ritchie_Architects" title="Ian Ritchie Architects">
         Ian Ritchie Architects
        </a>
        with the close up of the modern art tower
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/T._S._Eliot" title="T. S. Eliot">
       T. S. Eliot
      </a>
      made significant comments on the relation of the artist to tradition, including:
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <dl>
        <dd>
         "[W]e shall often find that not only the best, but the most individual parts of [a poet's] work, may be those in which the dead poets, his ancestors, assert their immortality most vigorously."
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eliot19_41-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-Eliot19-41">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           41
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </dd>
       </dl>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      However, relationship of modernism with tradition was complex, as literary scholar Peter Childs indicates:
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <dl>
        <dd>
         "There were paradoxical if not opposed trends towards revolutionary and reactionary positions, fear of the new and delight at the disappearance of the old, nihilism and fanatical enthusiasm, creativity and despair."
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Childs2000p17_10-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-Childs2000p17-10">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           10
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </dd>
       </dl>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      An example of how modernist art can be both revolutionary and yet be related to past tradition, is the music of the composer
      <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Schoenberg" title="Arnold Schoenberg">
       Arnold Schoenberg
      </a>
      . On the one hand Schoenberg rejected traditional
      <a href="/wiki/Tonality" title="Tonality">
       tonal harmony
      </a>
      , the hierarchical system of organizing works of music that had guided music making for at least a century and a half. He believed he had discovered a wholly new way of organizing sound, based in the use of
      <a href="/wiki/Twelve-tone_technique" title="Twelve-tone technique">
       twelve-note rows
      </a>
      . Yet while this was indeed wholly new, its origins can be traced back in the work of earlier composers, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Franz_Liszt" title="Franz Liszt">
       Franz Liszt
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">
       Richard Wagner
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Mahler" title="Gustav Mahler">
       Gustav Mahler
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_Strauss" title="Richard Strauss">
       Richard Strauss
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Max_Reger" title="Max Reger">
       Max Reger
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Furthermore it must be noted that Schoenberg also wrote tonal music throughout his career.
     </p>
     <p>
      In the world of art, in the first decade of the 20th century, young painters such as
      <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Picasso" title="Pablo Picasso">
       Pablo Picasso
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Henri_Matisse" title="Henri Matisse">
       Henri Matisse
      </a>
      were causing a shock with their rejection of traditional perspective as the means of structuring paintings,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      though the impressionist
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monet" title="Monet">
       Monet
      </a>
      had already been innovative in his use of perspective.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1907, as Picasso was painting
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon" title="Les Demoiselles d'Avignon">
        Les Demoiselles d'Avignon
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Oskar_Kokoschka" title="Oskar Kokoschka">
       Oskar Kokoschka
      </a>
      was writing
      <i>
       Mrder, Hoffnung der Frauen
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       Murderer, Hope of Women
      </i>
      ), the first Expressionist play (produced with scandal in 1909), and Arnold Schoenberg was composing his
      <i>
       String Quartet No.2 in F-sharp minor
      </i>
      (1908), his first composition without a tonal centre.
     </p>
     <p>
      A primary influence that led to Cubism was the representation of three-dimensional form in the late works of
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_C%C3%A9zanne" title="Paul Czanne">
       Paul Czanne
      </a>
      , which were displayed in a retrospective at the 1907
      <a href="/wiki/Salon_d%27Automne" title="Salon d'Automne">
       Salon d'Automne
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christopher_Green_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Christopher_Green-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In Cubist artwork, objects are analyzed, broken up and reassembled in an abstracted form and instead of depicting objects from one viewpoint, the artist depicts the subject from a multitude of viewpoints to represent the subject in a greater context.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Cubism" title="Cubism">
       Cubism
      </a>
      was brought to the attention of the general public for the first time in 1911 at the
      <a href="/wiki/Soci%C3%A9t%C3%A9_des_Artistes_Ind%C3%A9pendants" title="Socit des Artistes Indpendants">
       Salon des Indpendants
      </a>
      in Paris (held 21 April 13 June).
      <a href="/wiki/Jean_Metzinger" title="Jean Metzinger">
       Jean Metzinger
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Albert_Gleizes" title="Albert Gleizes">
       Albert Gleizes
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Henri_Le_Fauconnier" title="Henri Le Fauconnier">
       Henri Le Fauconnier
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Delaunay" title="Robert Delaunay">
       Robert Delaunay
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Fernand_L%C3%A9ger" title="Fernand Lger">
       Fernand Lger
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Roger_de_La_Fresnaye" title="Roger de La Fresnaye">
       Roger de La Fresnaye
      </a>
      were shown together in Room 41, provoking a 'scandal' out of which Cubism emerged and spread throughout Paris and beyond. Also in 1911,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kandinsky" title="Kandinsky">
       Kandinsky
      </a>
      painted
      <i>
       Bild mit Kreis
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       Picture With a Circle
      </i>
      ) which he later called the first abstract painting.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1912
      <a href="/wiki/Jean_Metzinger" title="Jean Metzinger">
       Jean Metzinger
      </a>
      and Albert Gleizes wrote the first (and only) major Cubist manifesto,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Du_%22Cubisme%22" title='Du "Cubisme"'>
        Du "Cubisme"
       </a>
      </i>
      , published in time for the Salon de la
      <a href="/wiki/Section_d%27Or" title="Section d'Or">
       Section d'Or
      </a>
      , the largest Cubist exhibition to date. In 1912 Metzinger painted and exhibited his enchanting
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/La_Femme_au_Cheval" title="La Femme au Cheval">
        La Femme au Cheval
       </a>
       (Woman with a horse)
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Danseuse au caf (
       <a href="/wiki/Dancer_in_a_caf%C3%A9" title="Dancer in a caf">
        Dancer in a caf
       </a>
       )
      </i>
      . Albert Gleizes painted and exhibited his
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Les_Baigneuses_(Gleizes)" title="Les Baigneuses (Gleizes)">
        Les Baigneuses (The Bathers)
       </a>
      </i>
      and his monumental
      <i>
       Le Dpiquage des Moissons (
       <a href="/wiki/Harvest_Threshing" title="Harvest Threshing">
        Harvest Threshing
       </a>
       )
      </i>
      . This work, along with
      <i>
       La Ville de Paris
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       City of Paris
      </i>
      ) by
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Delaunay" title="Robert Delaunay">
       Robert Delaunay
      </a>
      , is the largest and most ambitious
      <a href="/wiki/Cubism" title="Cubism">
       Cubist
      </a>
      painting undertaken during the pre-War Cubist period.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1905, a group of four German artists, led by
      <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Ludwig_Kirchner" title="Ernst Ludwig Kirchner">
       Ernst Ludwig Kirchner
      </a>
      , formed
      <a href="/wiki/Die_Br%C3%BCcke" title="Die Brcke">
       Die Brcke
      </a>
      (the Bridge) in the city of Dresden. This was arguably the founding organization for the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Expressionist" title="German Expressionist">
       German Expressionist
      </a>
      movement, though they did not use the word itself. A few years later, in 1911, a like-minded group of young artists formed
      <a href="/wiki/Der_Blaue_Reiter" title="Der Blaue Reiter">
       Der Blaue Reiter
      </a>
      (The Blue Rider) in Munich. The name came from
      <a href="/wiki/Wassily_Kandinsky" title="Wassily Kandinsky">
       Wassily Kandinsky
      </a>
      's Der Blaue Reiter painting of 1903. Among their members were Kandinsky,
      <a href="/wiki/Franz_Marc" title="Franz Marc">
       Franz Marc
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_Klee" title="Paul Klee">
       Paul Klee
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auguste_Macke" title="Auguste Macke">
       Auguste Macke
      </a>
      . However, the term "Expressionism" did not firmly establish itself until 1913.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Though initially mainly a German artistic movement,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      most predominant in painting, poetry and the theatre between 191030, most precursors of the movement were not German. Furthermore there have been expressionist writers of prose fiction, as well as non-German speaking expressionist writers, and, while the movement had declined in Germany with the rise of
      <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">
       Adolf Hitler
      </a>
      in the 1930s, there were subsequent expressionist works.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Egon_Schiele_061.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2007" data-file-width="2024" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Egon_Schiele_061.jpg/220px-Egon_Schiele_061.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Egon_Schiele_061.jpg/330px-Egon_Schiele_061.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Egon_Schiele_061.jpg/440px-Egon_Schiele_061.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Egon_Schiele_061.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         Portrait of Eduard Kosmack
        </i>
        by
        <a href="/wiki/Egon_Schiele" title="Egon Schiele">
         Egon Schiele
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Expressionism is notoriously difficult to define, in part because it "overlapped with other major 'isms' of the modernist period: with
      <a href="/wiki/Futurism" title="Futurism">
       Futurism
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Vorticism" title="Vorticism">
       Vorticism
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cubism" title="Cubism">
       Cubism
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Surrealism" title="Surrealism">
       Surrealism
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Dada" title="Dada">
       Dada
      </a>
      ."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Richard Murphy also comments: "the search for an all-inclusive definition is problematic to the extent that the most challenging expressionists" such as the novelist
      <a href="/wiki/Franz_Kafka" title="Franz Kafka">
       Franz Kafka
      </a>
      , poet
      <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Benn" title="Gottfried Benn">
       Gottfried Benn
      </a>
      , and novelist
      <a href="/wiki/Alfred_D%C3%B6blin" title="Alfred Dblin">
       Alfred Dblin
      </a>
      were simultaneously the most vociferous anti-expressionists.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      What, however, can be said, is that it was a movement that developed in the early twentieth-century mainly in Germany in reaction to the dehumanizing effect of industrialization and the growth of cities, and that "one of the central means by which expressionism identifies itself as an
      <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde" title="Avant-garde">
       avant-garde
      </a>
      movement, and by which it marks its distance to traditions and the cultural institution as a whole is through its relationship to
      <a href="/wiki/Realism_(arts)" title="Realism (arts)">
       realism
      </a>
      and the dominant conventions of representation."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      More explicitly: that the expressionists rejected the ideology of realism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NewYorkSeagram_04.30.2008.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4206" data-file-width="2736" height="338" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/NewYorkSeagram_04.30.2008.JPG/220px-NewYorkSeagram_04.30.2008.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/NewYorkSeagram_04.30.2008.JPG/330px-NewYorkSeagram_04.30.2008.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/NewYorkSeagram_04.30.2008.JPG/440px-NewYorkSeagram_04.30.2008.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NewYorkSeagram_04.30.2008.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Ludwig Mies van der Rohe's
        <a href="/wiki/Seagram_Building" title="Seagram Building">
         Seagram Building
        </a>
        in New York City
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      There was a concentrated Expressionist movement in early 20th century
      <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">
       German
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Theatre" title="Theatre">
       theatre
      </a>
      of which
      <a href="/wiki/Georg_Kaiser" title="Georg Kaiser">
       Georg Kaiser
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Toller" title="Ernst Toller">
       Ernst Toller
      </a>
      were the most famous playwrights. Other notable Expressionist dramatists included
      <a href="/wiki/Reinhard_Sorge" title="Reinhard Sorge">
       Reinhard Sorge
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Walter_Hasenclever" title="Walter Hasenclever">
       Walter Hasenclever
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hans_Henny_Jahnn" title="Hans Henny Jahnn">
       Hans Henny Jahnn
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Arnolt_Bronnen" title="Arnolt Bronnen">
       Arnolt Bronnen
      </a>
      . They looked back to Swedish playwright
      <a href="/wiki/August_Strindberg" title="August Strindberg">
       August Strindberg
      </a>
      and German actor and dramatist
      <a href="/wiki/Frank_Wedekind" title="Frank Wedekind">
       Frank Wedekind
      </a>
      as precursors of their
      <a href="/wiki/Dramaturgy" title="Dramaturgy">
       dramaturgical
      </a>
      experiments.
      <a href="/wiki/Oskar_Kokoschka" title="Oskar Kokoschka">
       Oskar Kokoschka
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Murderer,_the_Hope_of_Women" title="Murderer, the Hope of Women">
        Murderer, the Hope of Women
       </a>
      </i>
      was the first fully Expressionist work for the theatre, which opened on 4 July 1909 in
      <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">
       Vienna
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The extreme simplification of characters to mythic
      <a href="/wiki/Archetype" title="Archetype">
       types
      </a>
      , choral effects, declamatory dialogue and heightened intensity would become characteristic of later Expressionist plays. The first full-length Expressionist play was
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Son_(play)" title="The Son (play)">
        The Son
       </a>
      </i>
      by
      <a href="/wiki/Walter_Hasenclever" title="Walter Hasenclever">
       Walter Hasenclever
      </a>
      , which was published in 1914 and first performed in 1916.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Futurism_(art)" title="Futurism (art)">
       Futurism
      </a>
      is yet another modernist movement and in 1909, the Parisian newspaper
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Le_Figaro" title="Le Figaro">
        Le Figaro
       </a>
      </i>
      published
      <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Tommaso_Marinetti" title="Filippo Tommaso Marinetti">
       F. T. Marinetti
      </a>
      's first manifesto. Soon afterwards a group of painters (
      <a href="/wiki/Giacomo_Balla" title="Giacomo Balla">
       Giacomo Balla
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Umberto_Boccioni" title="Umberto Boccioni">
       Umberto Boccioni
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Carlo_Carr%C3%A0" title="Carlo Carr">
       Carlo Carr
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Russolo" title="Luigi Russolo">
       Luigi Russolo
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Gino_Severini" title="Gino Severini">
       Gino Severini
      </a>
      ) co-signed the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Futurist_Manifesto" title="Futurist Manifesto">
       Futurist Manifesto
      </a>
      . Modeled on
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marx" title="Marx">
       Marx
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Engels" title="Engels">
       Engels
      </a>
      ' famous "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communist_Manifesto" title="Communist Manifesto">
       Communist Manifesto
      </a>
      " (1848), such manifestoes put forward ideas that were meant to provoke and to gather followers. However, arguments in favor of geometric or purely abstract painting were, at this time, largely confined to "little magazines" which had only tiny circulations. Modernist primitivism and pessimism were controversial, and the mainstream in the first decade of the 20th century was still inclined towards a faith in progress and liberal optimism.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jean_Metzinger,_1913,_En_Canot,_oil_on_canvas,_146_x_114_cm,_missing_or_destroyed.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="578" data-file-width="453" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c5/Jean_Metzinger%2C_1913%2C_En_Canot%2C_oil_on_canvas%2C_146_x_114_cm%2C_missing_or_destroyed.jpg/220px-Jean_Metzinger%2C_1913%2C_En_Canot%2C_oil_on_canvas%2C_146_x_114_cm%2C_missing_or_destroyed.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c5/Jean_Metzinger%2C_1913%2C_En_Canot%2C_oil_on_canvas%2C_146_x_114_cm%2C_missing_or_destroyed.jpg/330px-Jean_Metzinger%2C_1913%2C_En_Canot%2C_oil_on_canvas%2C_146_x_114_cm%2C_missing_or_destroyed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c5/Jean_Metzinger%2C_1913%2C_En_Canot%2C_oil_on_canvas%2C_146_x_114_cm%2C_missing_or_destroyed.jpg/440px-Jean_Metzinger%2C_1913%2C_En_Canot%2C_oil_on_canvas%2C_146_x_114_cm%2C_missing_or_destroyed.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jean_Metzinger,_1913,_En_Canot,_oil_on_canvas,_146_x_114_cm,_missing_or_destroyed.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Jean_Metzinger" title="Jean Metzinger">
         Jean Metzinger
        </a>
        , 1913,
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/En_Canot" title="En Canot">
          En Canot (Im Boot)
         </a>
        </i>
        , oil on canvas, 146 x 114 cm (57.5 in  44.9 in), exhibited at Moderni Umeni,
        <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A1nes_Union_of_Fine_Arts" title="Mnes Union of Fine Arts">
         S.V.U. Mnes
        </a>
        , Prague, 1914, acquired in 1916 by
        <a href="/wiki/Georg_Muche" title="Georg Muche">
         Georg Muche
        </a>
        at the Galerie
        <a href="/wiki/Der_Sturm" title="Der Sturm">
         Der Sturm
        </a>
        , confiscated by the Nazis circa 193637, displayed at the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Degenerate_Art" title="Degenerate Art">
         Degenerate Art
        </a>
        show in Munich, and missing ever since.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
         <a href="#cite_note-59">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          59
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Abstract_art" title="Abstract art">
       Abstract
      </a>
      artists, taking as their examples the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Impressionists" title="Impressionists">
       impressionists
      </a>
      , as well as
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_C%C3%A9zanne" title="Paul Czanne">
       Paul Czanne
      </a>
      (18391906) and
      <a href="/wiki/Edvard_Munch" title="Edvard Munch">
       Edvard Munch
      </a>
      (18631944), began with the assumption that color and
      <a href="/wiki/Shape" title="Shape">
       shape
      </a>
      , not the depiction of the natural world, formed the essential characteristics of art.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_art" title="Western art">
       Western art
      </a>
      had been, from the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">
       Renaissance
      </a>
      up to the middle of the 19th century, underpinned by the logic of
      <a href="/wiki/Perspective_(visual)" title="Perspective (visual)">
       perspective
      </a>
      and an attempt to reproduce an illusion of visible reality. The arts of cultures other than the European had become accessible and showed alternative ways of describing visual experience to the artist. By the end of the 19th century many artists felt a need to create a new kind of art which would encompass the fundamental changes taking place in technology, science and philosophy. The sources from which individual artists drew their theoretical arguments were diverse, and reflected the social and intellectual preoccupations in all areas of Western culture at that time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Wassily_Kandinsky" title="Wassily Kandinsky">
       Wassily Kandinsky
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Piet_Mondrian" title="Piet Mondrian">
       Piet Mondrian
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Kazimir_Malevich" title="Kazimir Malevich">
       Kazimir Malevich
      </a>
      all believed in redefining art as the arrangement of pure color. The use of photography, which had rendered much of the representational function of visual art obsolete, strongly affected this aspect of modernism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-impressionism758_62-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-impressionism758-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
