      <a href="/wiki/Emergence" title="Emergence">
       emergent
      </a>
      effects. Many modernists came to this viewpoint, for example
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_Hindemith" title="Paul Hindemith">
       Paul Hindemith
      </a>
      in his late turn towards mysticism. Writers such as Paul H. Ray and Sherry Ruth Anderson, in
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Cultural_Creatives:_How_50_Million_People_Are_Changing_the_World" title="The Cultural Creatives: How 50 Million People Are Changing the World">
        The Cultural Creatives: How 50 Million People Are Changing the World
       </a>
      </i>
      (2000), Fredrick Turner in
      <i>
       A Culture of Hope
      </i>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lester_Brown" title="Lester Brown">
       Lester Brown
      </a>
      in
      <i>
       Plan B
      </i>
      , have articulated a critique of the basic idea of Modernism itself that individual creative expression should conform to the realities of technology. Instead, they argue, individual creativity should make everyday life more emotionally acceptable.
     </p>
     <p>
      Some traditionalist artists like
      <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Stoddart" title="Alexander Stoddart">
       Alexander Stoddart
      </a>
      reject Modernism generally as the product of "an epoch of false money allied with false culture".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141">
       <a href="#cite_note-141">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        141
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In some fields the effects of Modernism have remained stronger and more persistent than in others. Visual art has made the most complete break with its past. Most major capital cities have museums devoted to Modern Art as distinct from post-
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">
       Renaissance
      </a>
      art (
      <i>
       circa
      </i>
      1400 to
      <i>
       circa
      </i>
      1900). Examples include the
      <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Modern_Art" title="Museum of Modern Art">
       Museum of Modern Art
      </a>
      in New York, the
      <a href="/wiki/Tate_Modern" title="Tate Modern">
       Tate Modern
      </a>
      in London, and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centre_Pompidou" title="Centre Pompidou">
       Centre Pompidou
      </a>
      in Paris. These galleries make no distinction between modernist and postmodernist phases, seeing both as developments within Modern Art.
     </p>
     <h2>

      <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">

      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">

       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
      <ol class="references">
       <li id="cite_note-1">

         </a>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-2">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">

           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">

         </a>
         ,
         <i>

         </i>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University_Press" title="Cambridge University Press">
          Cambridge University Press
         </a>
         , 2000). pp 3839.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-3">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-3">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         "
         <a href="/wiki/James_Joyce" title="James Joyce">
          [James] Joyce
         </a>
         's
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_(novel)" title="Ulysses (novel)">
           Ulysses
          </a>
         </i>
         is a comedy not divine, ending, like Dante's, in the vision of a God whose will is our peace, but human all-too-human...." Peter Faulkner,

         </i>

        </span>
       </li>

       <li id="cite_note-Barth79Replenishment-5">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         ^
         <a href="#cite_ref-Barth79Replenishment_5-0">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             a
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-Barth79Replenishment_5-1">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Barth (1979) quotation:
        </span>
        <blockquote class="toccolours" style="float:none; display:table;">
         <div style="padding: 10px 15px 10px 15px;">
          <span class="reference-text">
           The ground motive of modernism, Graff asserts, was criticism of the nineteenth-century bourgeois social order and its world view. Its artistic strategy was the self-conscious overturning of the conventions of bourgeois realism [...] the antirationalist, antirealist, antibourgeois program of Modernism [...] the modernists, carrying the torch of romanticism, taught us that linearity, rationality, consciousness, cause and effect, nave illusionism, transparent language, innocent anecdote, and middle-class moral conventions are not the whole story
          </span>
         </div>
        </blockquote>
       </li>

       <li id="cite_note-Eco90-8">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         ^
         <a href="#cite_ref-Eco90_8-0">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             a
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-Eco90_8-1">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Eco (1990) p. 95 quote:
        </span>
        <blockquote class="toccolours" style="float:none; display:table;">
         <div style="padding: 10px 15px 10px 15px;">
          <span class="reference-text">
           Each of the types of repetition that we have examined is not limited to the mass media but belongs by right to the entire history of artistic creativity; plagiarism, quotation, parody, the ironic retake are typical of the entire artistic-literary tradition.
           <br/>
           Much art has been and is repetitive. The concept of absolute originality is a contemporary one, born with Romanticism; classical art was in vast measure serial, and the "modern" avant-garde (at the beginning of this century) challenged the Romantic idea of "creation from nothingness," with its techniques of collage, mustachios on the Mona Lisa, art about art, and so on.
          </span>
         </div>
        </blockquote>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-Steiner98p489-9">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         ^
         <a href="#cite_ref-Steiner98p489_9-0">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             a
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-Steiner98p489_9-1">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Steiner (1998) pp. 48990 quote:
        </span>
        <blockquote class="toccolours" style="float:none; display:table;">
         <div style="padding: 10px 15px 10px 15px;">
          <span class="reference-text">
           (pp. 48990) The modernist movement which dominated art, music, letters during the first half of the century was, at critical points, a strategy of conservation, of custodianship. Stravinsky's genius developed through phases of recapitulation. He took from Machaut, Gesualdo, Monteverdi. He mimed Tchaikovsky and Gounod, the Beethoven piano sonatas, the symphonies of Haydn, the operas of Pergolesi and Glinks. He incorporated Debussy and Webern into his own idiom. In each instance the listener was meant to recognize the source, to grasp the intent of a transformation which left salient aspects of the original intact. The history of Picasso is marked by retrospection. The explicit variations on classical pastoral themes, the citations from and
           <i>
            pastiches
           </i>
           of Rembrandt, Goya, Velzquez, Manet, are external products of a constant revision, a 'seeing again' in the light of technical and cultural shifts. Had we only Picasso's sculptures, graphics, and paintings, we could reconstruct a fair portion of the development of the arts from the Minoan to Czanne. In twentieth-century literature, the elements of reprise have been obsessive, and they have organized precisely those texts which at first seemed most revolutionary. 'The Waste Land',
           <i>
            Ulysses
           </i>
           , Pound's
           <i>
            Cantos
           </i>
           are deliberate assemblages, in-gatherings of a cultural past felt to be in danger of dissolution. The long sequence of imitations, translations, masked quotations, and explicit historical paintings in Robert Lowell's
           <i>
            History
           </i>
           has carried the same technique into the 1970s. [...] In Modernism
           <i>
            collage
           </i>
           has been the representative device. The new, even at its most scandalous, has been set against an informing background and framework of tradition. Stravinsky, Picasso, Braque, Eliot, Joyce, Poundthe 'makers of the new'have been neo-classics, often as observant of canonic precedent as their seventeenth-century forbears.
          </span>
         </div>
        </blockquote>
       </li>

       <li id="cite_note-11">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-11">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Everdell, William,
         <i>
          The First Moderns: Profiles in the Origin of Twentieth-Century Thought
         </i>
         . Chicago: University of Chicago Press, 1997,
         <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0226224805">
          ISBN 0226224805
         </a>
         .
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-12">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-12">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         "In the twentieth century, the social processes that bring this maelstrom into being, and keep it in a state of perpetual becoming, have come to be called 'modernization'. These world-historical processes have nourished an amazing variety of visions and ideas that aim to make men and women the subjects as well as the objects of modernization, to give them the power to change the world that is changing them, to make their way through the maelstrom and make it their own. Over the past century, these visions and values have come to be loosely grouped together under the name of 'modernism'" (Berman 1988, 16).
        </span>
       </li>

         (1).
         <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Serial_Number" title="International Standard Serial Number">
          ISSN
         </a>
         <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/1071-6068" rel="nofollow">
          1071-6068
         </a>
         .
        </span>
        <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AModernism&amp;rft.atitle=Obscene+Modernism+and+the+Trade+in+Salacious+Books&amp;rft.aufirst=Rachael&amp;rft.aulast=Potter&amp;rft.au=Potter%2C+Rachael&amp;rft.date=January+2009&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fmuse.jhu.edu%2Fjournals%2Fmodernism-modernity%2Fv016%2F16.1.potter.html&amp;rft.issn=1071-6068&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.jtitle=Modernism%2Fmodernity&amp;rft.pub=The+Johns+Hopkins+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=16">
         <span style="display:none;">
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li>
        Sass, Louis A. (1992).
        <i>
         Madness and Modernism: Insanity in the Light of Modern Art, Literature, and Thought
        </i>
        . New York: Basic Books. Cited in Bauer, Amy (2004). "Cognition, Constraints, and Conceptual Blends in Modernist Music", in
        <i>
         The Pleasure of Modernist Music
        </i>
        .
        <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1580461433">
         ISBN 1-58046-143-3
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carl_Schorske" title="Carl Schorske">
         Schorske, Carl
        </a>
        .
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fin-de-Si%C3%A8cle_Vienna:_Politics_and_Culture" title="Fin-de-Sicle Vienna: Politics and Culture">
          Fin-de-Sicle Vienna: Politics and Culture
         </a>
        </i>
        . Vintage, 1980. 978-0394744780.
       </li>
       <li>
        Schwartz, Sanford,
        <i>
         The Matrix of Modernism: Pound, Eliot, and Early Twentieth Century Thought,
        </i>
        Princeton, NJ:
        <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University_Press" title="Princeton University Press">

        , 1985

        <i>
         Gorge(l)

        .
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Museum_of_Fine_Arts,_Antwerp" title="Royal Museum of Fine Arts, Antwerp">

        <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9076979359">
